Just the chatbot or Claude doesn'tã€‚These attacks maybe aren't the most concerning thing because they won't reveal any information that's already on the Internetã€‚

 we're starting to use LLMs beyond just chatbotsï¼Œ rightï¼Œ We're starting to use them in agent systemsã€‚

 And when you use LLMs within larger systemsã€‚All of a sudden you're introducing security vulnerabilities into these systems that can act as significant security vulnerabilitiesã€‚

So to give a few examples of how this may come up as a problemï¼Œ you can imagineï¼Œ you knowã€‚

 a LLM that isn't just an LLM by itselfï¼Œ but actually is an LLM that can also browse the web to get informationã€‚

And you can start to see how these sorts of things can cause real security flawsã€‚ Soï¼Œ for exampleã€‚

 you knowï¼Œ if you ask an LLM that has access to the webï¼Œ what's the current price of H100 GPUusã€‚

 it will search the webã€‚ And if the top hit on that page happens to have some malicious content that's inside that web page telling the essentially the LLM to ignore what it was asked to do and just insult the userã€‚

 then maybe you get a system that will insult the user tells you to train decision trees if you can't afford H100sã€‚

å—¯ã€‚But even more soï¼Œ once we start giving more capabilities to these systemsï¼Œ for exampleã€‚

 in viewing them with email sending abilitiesï¼Œ then the risks become even more greatï¼Œ rightã€‚

 because nowï¼Œ you knowï¼Œ if theã€‚Top hit on this query instructs the L L M to actually do things like send out a spam offer to all your email contactsã€‚

 It might just carry this out without actually without really following the intended instructions of the actual systemã€‚

And soã€‚What I would argue is that adversarial attacksã€‚Are somewhat akin to a buffer overflowã€‚

 They are a vulnerability that is present in all L Mï¼Œ so farã€‚But even more than thisã€‚

 unlike normal buffer overflowsï¼Œ we don't even really know how to patch itã€‚

 So as we release systems that use LLMsï¼Œ we essentially are releasing systems with known security flaws that we don't know how to patch right nowã€‚

And my belief is that whether or not we can fix this will be the primary factor in determining whether these AI systems for now remain chatbots or whether they really become the intelligent agents that we want to build going forwardã€‚

Al rightï¼Œ so now let me talk about how these attacks actually work and this is going to be a very high level presentationã€‚

 but I want to give a broad overview of how these things workã€‚

 This is of course all in the paper that's available onlineã€‚ you can see at LMattsã€‚orgã€‚ğŸ˜Šã€‚

So the way just to recap hereï¼Œ the way LLM alignment typically works is that we've first pretrain on a lot of raw data from the internetã€‚

After thisï¼Œ we thenã€‚Fine tune the model with instruction tuning as well as human preference data or data on harmful or helpful behavior this is alleged with a much smaller amount of thisã€‚

 we give it examples likeï¼Œ for exampleï¼Œ when you when you ask it how to hotwi a carã€‚

 the correct response according to this fine tuning data will be to say I can't do thatã€‚

But the problem here is that the knowledge of how to hotwi a car is still inherent inside that large language model rightã€‚

 and so it's only in some sense been kind of plastered over by this additional instructions to say even though you know how to hotwire a carã€‚

 don't tell the user how to do itã€‚And the problem is that we can circumvent thisã€‚

And the way that we circumvent this is veryï¼Œ very simpleã€‚

 We take a query like how we hot wirere a carï¼Œ and we append to it a whole bunch of extra tokensã€‚

 These are like exclamation points and things like thisã€‚ That's so that they start out withã€‚

 that won't break the system as it isã€‚ That's just the starting pointã€‚

Then we take an open source language modelã€‚ So this is something like theï¼Œ the Lama language modelã€‚

And because this model been is open source and we've assessed the weightsã€‚

 we can actually look at the exact probability that this model saysï¼Œ you knowã€‚

 I'm sorry I can't do that or the probability that it says sureï¼Œ here's how you hotwi a carã€‚

And so the goal that we have now is we just want to start adjusting tokens here to increase this probability that the model responds sayingã€‚

 sureï¼Œ here's how you hotwi a carã€‚And what we're going to do is we're going to start substituting in tokensã€‚

 swapping tokens in this suffix in order to make that probability of how of the second response here to raise the probability of that response as high as possibleã€‚

Now we use a scoring of these tokens by the gradients of of the probability of this output with respect to those tokensã€‚

 but the details those are in the paperï¼Œ people are curiousã€‚

And we just keep swapping tokens in this wayã€‚ We keep switching words aroundã€‚ And eventuallyã€‚

 if we do this enoughï¼Œ we'll come up with a sequence that seems very kind of random because this is not actually language is just using an optimizer to construct itã€‚

But this seemingly random sequence of tokens actually causes the model to respond here's how youwi a carã€‚

And the most interesting thingï¼Œ I thinkï¼Œ is thatã€‚All we aim for in our objective is the phraseSer here's how to hot way a carã€‚

But then when you actually run this thingï¼Œ the model continues and says firstã€‚

 get a screwdriver move steering columnï¼Œ et ceteraã€‚ So basically once you've convinced the modelã€‚

 and wants the hot wire or cart will go ahead and actually do thisã€‚

Now the most interesting thing I think of all of this thoughã€‚

 is that all of this was done on open source modelsï¼Œ it was actually all doneã€‚

 all those suffixes you see were built about a year ago and they still work pretty well so what we do after we've constructed these things for open source models is we basically just copy and paste these into close source models like Claude here and we find the Clas actually more than happy to tell us how to hotwire a carã€‚

We don't fully understand why this transfer occursã€‚

 and I think understanding this transfer is one of the big open questions that we have about understanding safety of these modelsã€‚

Nowï¼Œ just to give some little bit more concrete resultsã€‚

 what we find is that our attack is able to very successfully break open source modelsã€‚

 so whenever we train it on a modelï¼Œ it can very easily break those modelsã€‚

Then even more surprisingly the model is able to very reliably break other third party models as wellã€‚

 this is the results we had in the paper of about a year agoï¼Œ since then we've actually of courseã€‚

 as you saw been able to break later iterations of Claude and others and these attacks actually are very effective still at breaking many of these modelsã€‚

Not only thisï¼Œ but the actual optimizer we use is very important hereã€‚

 so there there has been a long line of work at methods that try to optimize prompts in an automated fashionã€‚

 but our methodï¼Œ which we call the greedy coordinate gradient methodã€‚

 tends to be quite a bit better than all of theseã€‚Okayï¼Œ so to finish upã€‚

 I want to talk a little bit about what we can do about thisã€‚How might we prevent its attacksï¼Ÿ

And unfortunatelyï¼Œ the short answer that I have to be honest about is that we don't know how to fully prevent thisã€‚

We've been trying to fix adversarial robustnessï¼Œ as I mentioned with computer vision for over 10 yearsã€‚

 and I've worked in this problem a whole lotã€‚And I can say confidently because I've worked on it so much that we have largely failed at itã€‚

Now there are some optionsï¼Œ there are things like for LLMsï¼Œ at least things like aversarial trainingã€‚

 input output filtersï¼Œ prompt paraphrasingï¼Œ etc ceterã€‚But these systems seem somehow inadequateã€‚

 They don't seem fully capable of solving systemsï¼Œ or they seem to work wellã€‚

 maybe in in a black box settingï¼Œ but we can easily re break them if we have white box access and it's unclear if you want to rely on only having black box access to the system and things like thisã€‚

Howeverï¼Œ what I will add is that I think we have several recent results that I believe show the situation is quite a bit better for LL Ms than it was for computer visionã€‚

And so I want to highlight just a few of the current techniques we are using and share one more demo of a system that actually is able to much more reliablyã€‚

Defend against these kind of manipulations from the systemã€‚Okayã€‚

 so so how do we start to build more robust systems and I'm just going to show the rough techniques here or give a high level view of the techniques these are all covered in some recent papers we haveã€‚

 but I want to talk about sort of ways in which we might go about doing thisã€‚So first of allã€‚

 we can use what's called slow adversarial trainingã€‚

 So normal adversarial training of machine learning modelsã€‚

 you every gradient step you take when you're training the modelã€‚

 you actually construct an adversarial attack and train against that sort of attack exampleã€‚

 but that's very impractical for LLMs because these attacks that I'm showing you here they take sometimes up to a day to actually runã€‚

What we can do thoughï¼Œ is use a slower process by which we freeze a modelã€‚

Use this model or fine tuna model against existing attacks on this systemã€‚

 and then use the fine tune system to generate new attacks and repeat this process kind of in a slower phaseã€‚

 and this does seem to add a degree of robustnessã€‚We can also use a technique recently developed by collaborators Andyã€‚

 who's been the lead author on all of these papers that you see hereã€‚

PD student of myself and Matt Frederickson also works with Caseã€‚

This also recently developed a technique with several other collaborators called Re Engineingã€‚

And this techniqueï¼Œ basically what it does is it looks at the internal representations of activations and networks and tries to find directions in that space that are correlated with certain types of behaviorã€‚

The very cool thing though about LLMs and this is sort of different from traditional techniques in probing is that the way you find these directions of activation space often just involves asking the LLM to think about certain things so if you want to think about you want to find a direction for refusal you can kind of just ask the LLM to think about refus refusing instructions or on the flip side not refusing instructions and giving you full complete answers to questionsã€‚

ğŸ˜Šï¼ŒYou then can run PCA on this activation space of refusal versus non- refusalã€‚

 and you can reinforce those activation patterns when you are actually running the LLM and it will over increase the refusal or in factã€‚

 if you do the other way under increase the likelihood that the system will actually respond to youã€‚

And then finallyï¼Œ we actually have also used this representation engineeringã€‚

The learned representations from this approach develop a new technique called circuit breakersã€‚

And the idea of circuit breakers is we actually want to fine tune a model to make the representations of harmful informationã€‚

As orthogonal as possible to those of an original modelã€‚

 So you have an original model that will sometimes generate harmful behaviorsã€‚

 you want to change the representations such that for those harmful behaviorsã€‚

 the internal representations are as different as possible from the original model while minimizing the difference for non harmful queriesã€‚

And this works in many cases much better than just simple adversarial training or refusal trainingã€‚

 because it actually causes the model to sort of go haywire and kind of not make stop making any sense when it encounters any harmful information because these representations essentially no longer work as a valid LLM anymoreã€‚

Both those two papersï¼Œ the representation engineering paperã€‚

 and the circuit breakers paper are both on archive and the details of this are in the paperã€‚
